      <p>The <b>House of Tudor</b> was an English <a href="/wiki/Royal_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal house">royal house</a> of <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a> origin,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> descended from the <a href="/wiki/Tudors_of_Penmynydd" title="Tudors of Penmynydd">Tudors of Penmynydd</a>. Tudor monarchs ruled the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> and its realms, including their ancestral <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</a> (later the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a>) from 1485 until 1603, with five monarchs in that period: <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a>. (<a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a>, arguably queen for 9 days in 1553, descended from the House of Tudor in the female line). The Tudors succeeded the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">House of Plantagenet</a> as rulers of the Kingdom of England, and were succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a>. The first Tudor monarch, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII of England</a>, descended through his mother from a legitimised branch of the English royal <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">House of Lancaster</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tudors_of_Penmynydd" title="Tudors of Penmynydd">Tudor family</a> rose to power in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a> (1455-1487), which left the House of Lancaster, to which the Tudors were aligned, extinct in the male line. </p>
      <p>Henry Tudor, the future Henry VII, succeeded in presenting himself as a candidate not only for traditional Lancastrian supporters, but also for discontented supporters of their rival <a href="/wiki/House_of_York" title="House of York">House of York</a>, and he took the throne by <a href="/wiki/Right_of_conquest" title="Right of conquest">right of conquest</a>. Following his victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a> (22 August 1485), he reinforced his position in 1486 by fulfilling his 1483 vow to marry the English princess <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_York" title="Elizabeth of York">Elizabeth of York</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward IV">Edward IV</a>, thus symbolically uniting the former warring factions under the new dynasty. The Tudors extended their power beyond modern England, achieving the full union of England and the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Wales" title="Principality of Wales">Principality of Wales</a> in 1542 (<a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535_and_1542" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542">Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542</a>), and successfully asserting English authority over the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> (proclaimed by the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Ireland_Act_1542" title="Crown of Ireland Act 1542">Crown of Ireland Act 1542</a>). They also maintained the nominal English claim to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">Kingdom of France</a>; although none of them made substance of it, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> fought wars with France trying to reclaim that title. After him, his daughter <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I</a> lost control of all territory in France permanently with the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Calais_(1558)" title="Siege of Calais (1558)">fall of Calais</a> in 1558. </p>
      <p>In total, the Tudor monarchs ruled their domains for just over a century. Henry VIII (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reign">r</abbr>.&nbsp;1509–1547</span>) was the only son of Henry VII to live to the age of maturity. Issues around royal succession (including marriage and the succession rights of women) became major political themes during the Tudor era. When Elizabeth I died without an heir, the Scottish <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a> succeeded as England's royal family through the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a> of 24 March 1603. The first Stuart to become King of England (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reign">r</abbr>.&nbsp;1603–1625</span>), <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">James VI and I</a>, descended from Henry VII's daughter <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret Tudor</a>, who in 1503 had married King <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV of Scotland</a> in accordance with the 1502 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perpetual_Peace" title="Treaty of Perpetual Peace">Treaty of Perpetual Peace</a>. </p>
      <p>For analysis of politics, diplomacy and social history, see <a href="/wiki/Tudor_period" title="Tudor period">Tudor period</a>. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascent_to_the_throne">Ascent to the throne</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ascent to the throne">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The Tudors descended on <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a>'s mother's side from <a href="/wiki/John_Beaufort,_1st_Earl_of_Somerset" title="John Beaufort, 1st Earl of Somerset">John Beaufort, 1st Earl of Somerset</a>, one of the illegitimate children of the 14th century English prince <a href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt" title="John of Gaunt">John of Gaunt</a> (the third surviving son of <a href="/wiki/Edward_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward III">Edward III</a>) by Gaunt's long-term mistress <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Swynford" title="Katherine Swynford">Katherine Swynford</a>. The descendants of an illegitimate child of English royalty would normally have no claim on the throne, but the situation became complicated when Gaunt and Swynford eventually married in 1396, when John Beaufort was 25. The church retroactively declared the Beauforts legitimate by way of a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> the same year, confirmed by an <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Act of Parliament</a> in 1397. A subsequent proclamation by John of Gaunt's legitimate son, <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry IV</a>, also recognised the Beauforts' legitimacy but declared them ineligible ever to inherit the throne. Nevertheless, the Beauforts remained closely allied with Gaunt's legitimate descendants from his first marriage, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">House of Lancaster</a>. However the descent from the Beauforts, despite the above, did not render Henry of Richmond a legitimate heir to the throne nor did the fact that his father's mother had been a Queen of England make him an heir. The legitimate heir, or, in this case, heiress, was the Countess of Salisbury who was descended from the second son of Edward III, Lionel, Duke of Clarence and also his fourth son, the Duke of York. This is verified by the Tudor family tree which appears later in this article. Henry Tudor had, however, one thing that the others did not. He had an army which had defeated and killed the last Yorkist King, Richard III and therefore the support of powerful nobles. His son Henry VIII made sure there were no other claimants to the Throne when he wiped out all the remaining Plantagenet heirs including the Countess of Salisbury and her family the Poles. One Pole alone survived but he was a cardinal in the Catholic Church. He later became Archbishop of Canterbury under the Catholic Mary I. </p>
      <p>On 1 November 1455, John Beaufort's granddaughter, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Beaufort,_Countess_of_Richmond_and_Derby" class="mw-redirect" title="Margaret Beaufort, Countess of Richmond and Derby">Margaret Beaufort, Countess of Richmond and Derby</a>, married <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI</a>'s maternal half-brother <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Tudor,_1st_Earl_of_Richmond" title="Edmund Tudor, 1st Earl of Richmond">Edmund Tudor, 1st Earl of Richmond</a>. It was his father, <a href="/wiki/Owen_Tudor" title="Owen Tudor">Owen Tudor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>: <i lang="cy">Owain ap Maredudd ap Tudur ap Goronwy ap Tudur ap Goronwy ap Ednyfed Fychan</i>), who abandoned the Welsh <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymic</a> naming practice and adopted a fixed surname. When he did, he did not choose, as was generally the custom, his father's name, Maredudd, but chose that of his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Tudur_ap_Goronwy" title="Tudur ap Goronwy">Tudur ap Goronwy</a>, instead. This name is sometimes given as <i>Tewdwr</i>, the Welsh form of <a href="/wiki/Theodore_(name)" title="Theodore (name)">Theodore</a>, but Modern Welsh <i>Tudur</i>, Old Welsh <i>Tutir</i> is originally not a variant but a different and completely unrelated name, etymologically identical with <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">Gaulish</a> <i>Toutorix</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic_language" title="Proto-Celtic language">Proto-Celtic</a> <i>*toutā</i> "people, tribe" and <i>*rīxs</i> "king" (compare Modern Welsh <i>tud</i> "territory" and <i>rhi</i> "king"<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-3">[3]</a></sup> respectively), corresponding to Germanic <a href="/wiki/Theodoric" title="Theodoric">Theodoric</a>. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Owen_Tudor" title="Owen Tudor">Owen Tudor</a> was one of the bodyguards for the <a href="/wiki/Queen_dowager" title="Queen dowager">queen dowager</a> <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Valois" title="Catherine of Valois">Catherine of Valois</a>, whose husband, <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a>, had died in 1422. Evidence suggests that the two were secretly married in 1429. The two sons born of the marriage, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Tudor,_1st_Earl_of_Richmond" title="Edmund Tudor, 1st Earl of Richmond">Edmund</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jasper_Tudor,_Duke_of_Bedford" class="mw-redirect" title="Jasper Tudor, Duke of Bedford">Jasper</a>, were among the most loyal supporters of the House of Lancaster in its struggle against the House of York. Henry VI ennobled his half-brothers: Edmund became <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Richmond" title="Earl of Richmond">Earl of Richmond</a> on 15 December 1449<sup id="cite_ref-Griffith,_Ralph_A_1985_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffith,_Ralph_A_1985-4">[4]</a></sup> and was married to <a href="/wiki/Lady_Margaret_Beaufort" title="Lady Margaret Beaufort">Lady Margaret Beaufort</a>, the great-granddaughter of John of Gaunt, the progenitor of the house of Lancaster; Jasper became the first <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Pembroke" title="Earl of Pembroke">Earl of Pembroke</a> on 23 November 1452.<sup id="cite_ref-Griffith,_Ralph_A_1985_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffith,_Ralph_A_1985-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Tudor,_1st_Earl_of_Richmond" title="Edmund Tudor, 1st Earl of Richmond">Edmund</a> died on 3 November 1456. On 28 January 1457, his widow Margaret, who had just attained her fourteenth birthday, gave birth to a son, Henry Tudor, at her brother-in-law's <a href="/wiki/Pembroke_Castle" title="Pembroke Castle">Pembroke Castle</a>. </p>
      <p>Henry Tudor, the future Henry VII, spent his childhood at <a href="/wiki/Raglan_Castle" title="Raglan Castle">Raglan Castle</a>, the home of <a href="/wiki/William_Herbert,_1st_Earl_of_Pembroke_(died_1469)" title="William Herbert, 1st Earl of Pembroke (died 1469)">William Herbert, 1st Earl of Pembroke</a>, a leading Yorkist. Following the murder of Henry VI and death of his son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_of_Westminster,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Edward of Westminster, Prince of Wales">Edward</a>, in 1471, Henry became the person upon whom the Lancastrian cause rested. Concerned for his young nephew's life, Jasper Tudor took Henry to <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> for safety. Lady Margaret remained in England and remarried, living quietly while advancing the Lancastrian (and her son's) cause. Capitalizing on the growing unpopularity of <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III</a> (King of England from 1483), she was able to forge an alliance with discontented Yorkists in support of her son. Two years after Richard III was crowned, Henry and Jasper sailed from the mouth of the Seine to the <a href="/wiki/Milford_Haven_Waterway" title="Milford Haven Waterway">Milford Haven Waterway</a> and defeated Richard III at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a> (22 August 1485).<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-3">[3]</a></sup> Upon this victory, Henry Tudor proclaimed himself King Henry VII. </p> 
      <table class="collapsible collapsed" style="margin: 0.3em auto auto; clear:none; min-width:45em; width:100%; max-width:1024px; font-size:88%; border:1px solid #aaa;">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="All_about_Henry_VII">All about Henry VII</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: All about Henry VII">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Seven_England.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Henry_Seven_England.jpg/170px-Henry_Seven_England.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Henry_Seven_England.jpg/255px-Henry_Seven_England.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Henry_Seven_England.jpg/340px-Henry_Seven_England.jpg 2x" data-file-width="408" data-file-height="589"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Seven_England.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">King Henry VII</a>, the founder of the royal house of Tudor
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Upon becoming king in 1485, Henry VII moved rapidly to secure his hold on the throne. On 18 January 1486 at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster</a>, he honoured a pledge made three years earlier and married <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_York" title="Elizabeth of York">Elizabeth of York</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Williams25_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams25-5">[5]</a></sup> (daughter of King Edward IV). They were third cousins, as both were great-great-grandchildren of John of Gaunt. The marriage unified the warring houses of Lancaster and York and gave the couple's children a strong claim to the throne. The unification of the two houses through this marriage is symbolized by the heraldic emblem of the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor rose</a>, a combination of the white rose of York and the red rose of Lancaster. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_York" title="Elizabeth of York">Elizabeth of York</a> had several children, four of whom survived infancy: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Arthur,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Arthur, Prince of Wales">Arthur, Prince of Wales</a> (born 1486, died 1502)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry, Duke of York</a> (born 1491, died 1547)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret</a> (born 1489, died 1541), who married <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV of Scotland</a></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Mary_Tudor_(queen_consort_of_France)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Tudor (queen consort of France)">Mary</a> (born 1496, died 1533), who married <a href="/wiki/Louis_XII_of_France" title="Louis XII of France">Louis XII of France</a></li>
      </ul> 
      <p>Henry VII's foreign policy had an objective of dynastic security: witness the alliance forged with the marriage in 1503 of his daughter Margaret to James IV of Scotland and through the marriage of his eldest son. In 1501 Henry VII married his son Arthur to <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>, cementing an alliance with the Spanish monarchs, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand II of Aragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a>. The newlyweds spent their honeymoon at <a href="/wiki/Ludlow_Castle" title="Ludlow Castle">Ludlow Castle</a>, the traditional seat of the <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinney_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinney-6">[6]</a></sup> However, four months after the marriage, Arthur died, leaving his younger brother <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry</a> as heir apparent. Henry VII acquired a <a href="/wiki/Papal_dispensation" class="mw-redirect" title="Papal dispensation">papal dispensation</a> allowing Prince Henry to marry Arthur's widow; however, Henry VII delayed the marriage. </p>
      <p>Henry VII limited his involvement in European politics. He went to war only twice: once in 1489 during the <a href="/wiki/French%E2%80%93Breton_War" title="French–Breton War">Breton crisis</a> and the invasion of Brittany, and in 1496–1497 in revenge for Scottish support of <a href="/wiki/Perkin_Warbeck" title="Perkin Warbeck">Perkin Warbeck</a> and for the Scottish invasion of northern England. Henry VII made peace with France in 1492 and the war against Scotland was abandoned because of the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_rebellion_of_1497" title="Cornish rebellion of 1497">Western Rebellion of 1497</a>. Henry VII came to peace with James IV in 1502, paving the way for the marriage of his daughter Margaret.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinney_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinney-6">[6]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>One of the main concerns of Henry VII during his reign was the re-accumulation of the funds in the royal treasury. England had never been one of the wealthier European countries, and after the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Roses" class="mw-redirect" title="War of the Roses">War of the Roses</a> this was even more true. Through his strict monetary strategy, he was able to leave a considerable amount of money in the Treasury for his son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a>. Although it is debated whether Henry VII was a great king, he certainly was a successful one if only because he restored the nation's finances, strengthened the judicial system and successfully denied all other claimants to the throne, thus further securing it for his heir.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_VIII">Henry VIII</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Henry VIII">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Catalina_de_Arag%C3%B3n,_por_un_artista_an%C3%B3nimo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Catalina_de_Arag%C3%B3n%2C_por_un_artista_an%C3%B3nimo.jpg/170px-Catalina_de_Arag%C3%B3n%2C_por_un_artista_an%C3%B3nimo.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Catalina_de_Arag%C3%B3n%2C_por_un_artista_an%C3%B3nimo.jpg/255px-Catalina_de_Arag%C3%B3n%2C_por_un_artista_an%C3%B3nimo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Catalina_de_Arag%C3%B3n%2C_por_un_artista_an%C3%B3nimo.jpg/340px-Catalina_de_Arag%C3%B3n%2C_por_un_artista_an%C3%B3nimo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1294" data-file-height="1682"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Catalina_de_Arag%C3%B3n,_por_un_artista_an%C3%B3nimo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>: marriage was annulled - by the Church of England - for not producing a male heir to the Tudor dynasty
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The new King <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> married <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a> on 11 June 1509; they were crowned at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> on 24 June the same year. Catherine was Henry's older brother's wife, making the path for their marriage a rocky one from the start. A papal dispensation had to be granted for Henry to be able to marry Catherine, and the negotiations took some time. Despite the fact that Henry's father died before he was married to Catherine, he was determined to marry her anyway and make sure that everyone knew he intended on being his own master. </p>
      <p>When Henry first came to the throne, he had very little interest in actually ruling; rather, he preferred to indulge in luxuries and to partake in sports. He let others control the kingdom for the first two years of his reign, and then when he became more interested in military strategy, he took more interest in ruling his own throne.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In his younger years, Henry was described as a man of gentle friendliness, gentle in debate, and who acted as more of a companion than a king. He was generous in his gifts and affection and was said to be easy to get along with. The Henry that many people picture when they hear his name is the Henry of his later years, when he became obese, volatile, and was known for his great cruelty.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Catherine did not bear Henry the sons he was desperate for; her first child, a daughter, was stillborn, and her second child, a son named <a href="/wiki/Henry,_Duke_of_Cornwall" title="Henry, Duke of Cornwall">Henry, Duke of Cornwall</a>, died 52 days after birth. A further set of stillborn children were conceived, until a daughter <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary</a> was born in 1516. When it became clear to Henry that the Tudor line was at risk, he consulted his chief minister <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_(Catholicism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal (Catholicism)">Cardinal</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolsey" title="Thomas Wolsey">Thomas Wolsey</a> about the possibility of annulling his marriage to Catherine. Along with Henry's concern that he would not have an heir, it was also obvious to his court that he was becoming tired of his aging wife, who was six years older than he was. Wolsey visited Rome, where he hoped to get the Pope's consent for an annulment. However, the church was reluctant to rescind the earlier papal dispensation and felt heavy pressure from Catherine's nephew, <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor</a>, in support of his aunt. Catherine contested the proceedings, and a protracted legal battle followed. Wolsey fell from favour as a result of his failure to procure the annulment, and Henry appointed <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell,_1st_Earl_of_Essex" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Cromwell, 1st Earl of Essex">Thomas Cromwell</a> in his place. </p>
      <p>Despite his failure to produce the results that Henry wanted, Wolsey actively pursued the annulment — divorce was synonymous with annulment at that time. However, he never planned that Henry would marry Anne Boleyn, with whom the king had become enamoured while she was lady-in-waiting in Queen Catherine's household. It is unclear how far Wolsey was actually responsible for the Reformation, but it is very clear that Henry's desire to marry <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a> precipitated the schism with the Church. Henry's concern about having an heir to secure his family line and increase his security while alive would have prompted him to ask for a divorce sooner or later, whether Anne had precipitated it or not. Only Wolsey's sudden death at <a href="/wiki/Leicester" title="Leicester">Leicester</a><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> on his journey to the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> saved him from the public humiliation and inevitable execution he would have suffered upon his arrival at the Tower.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Break_with_Rome">Break with Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Break with Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell,Thomas(1EEssex)01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/170px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/255px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/340px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2768" data-file-height="3312"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell,Thomas(1EEssex)01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell,_1st_Earl_of_Essex" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Cromwell, 1st Earl of Essex">Thomas Cromwell, 1st Earl of Essex</a>, Henry VIII's chief minister responsible for the 
         <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In order to allow Henry to divorce his wife and marry Anne Boleyn, the English parliament enacted laws breaking ties with Rome, and declaring the king Supreme Head of the Church of England (from <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth I">Elizabeth I</a> the monarch is known as the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Governor of the Church of England</a>), thus severing the ecclesiastical structure of England from the Catholic Church and the Pope. The newly appointed <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a>, was then able to declare Henry's marriage to Catherine <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulled</a>. Catherine was removed from Court, and she spent the last three years of her life in various English houses under "protectorship," similar to house arrest.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> This allowed Henry to marry one of his courtiers: Anne Boleyn, the daughter of a minor diplomat Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Boleyn,_1st_Earl_of_Wiltshire" title="Thomas Boleyn, 1st Earl of Wiltshire">Thomas Boleyn</a>. Anne had become pregnant by the end of 1532 and gave birth on 7 September 1533 to <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth</a>, named in honour of Henry's mother.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Anne may have had later pregnancies which ended in miscarriage or stillbirth. In May 1536, Anne was arrested, along with six courtiers. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell,_1st_Earl_of_Essex" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Cromwell, 1st Earl of Essex">Thomas Cromwell</a> stepped in again, claiming that Anne had taken lovers during her marriage to Henry, and she was tried for <a href="/wiki/High_treason" class="mw-redirect" title="High treason">high treason</a> and <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incest</a>; these charges were most likely fabricated, but she was found guilty, and executed in May 1536. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_alliance">Protestant alliance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Protestant alliance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Henry-VIII-kingofengland_1491-1547.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Henry-VIII-kingofengland_1491-1547.jpg/170px-Henry-VIII-kingofengland_1491-1547.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Henry-VIII-kingofengland_1491-1547.jpg/255px-Henry-VIII-kingofengland_1491-1547.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Henry-VIII-kingofengland_1491-1547.jpg/340px-Henry-VIII-kingofengland_1491-1547.jpg 2x" data-file-width="813" data-file-height="1400"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Henry-VIII-kingofengland_1491-1547.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a>: Henry's quarrels with the Pope led to the creation of the 
         <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Henry married again, for the third time, to <a href="/wiki/Jane_Seymour" title="Jane Seymour">Jane Seymour</a>, the daughter of a Wiltshire knight, and with whom he had become enamoured while she was still a lady-in-waiting to Queen Anne. Jane became pregnant, and in 1537 produced a son, who became <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">King Edward VI</a> following Henry's death in 1547. Jane died of <a href="/wiki/Puerperal_fever" class="mw-redirect" title="Puerperal fever">puerperal fever</a> only a few days after the birth, leaving Henry devastated. Cromwell continued to gain the king's favour when he designed and pushed through the <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535-1542" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535-1542">Laws in Wales Acts</a>, uniting England and Wales. </p>
      <p>In 1540, Henry married for the fourth time to the daughter of a Protestant German duke, <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Cleves" title="Anne of Cleves">Anne of Cleves</a>, thus forming an alliance with the Protestant German states. Henry was reluctant to marry again, especially to a Protestant, but he was persuaded when the court painter <a href="/wiki/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger" title="Hans Holbein the Younger">Hans Holbein the Younger</a> showed him a flattering portrait of her. She arrived in England in December 1539, and Henry rode to <a href="/wiki/Rochester,_Kent" title="Rochester, Kent">Rochester</a> to meet her on 1 January 1540. Although the historian <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Burnet" title="Gilbert Burnet">Gilbert Burnet</a> claimed that Henry called her a <i>Flanders Mare</i>, there is no evidence that he said this; in truth, court ambassadors negotiating the marriage praised her beauty. Whatever the circumstances were, the marriage failed, and Anne agreed to a peaceful annulment, assumed the title <i>My Lady, the King's Sister</i>, and received a massive divorce settlement, which included <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Palace" title="Richmond Palace">Richmond Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hever_Castle" title="Hever Castle">Hever Castle</a>, and numerous other estates across the country. Although the marriage made sense in terms of foreign policy, Henry was still enraged and offended by the match. Henry chose to blame Cromwell for the failed marriage, and ordered him beheaded on 28 July 1540.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Henry kept his word and took care of Anne in his last years alive; however, after his death Anne suffered from extreme financial hardship because Edward VI's councillors refused to give her any funds and confiscated the homes she had been given. She pleaded to her brother to let her return home, but he only sent a few agents who tried to assist in helping her situation and refused to let her return home. Anne died on 16 July 1557 in <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Manor" title="Chelsea Manor">Chelsea Manor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/170px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/255px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/340px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3129"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a>, Henry's first Protestant 
         <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, responsible for the 
         <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> during Edward VI's reign
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The fifth marriage was to the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Howard" title="Catherine Howard">Catherine Howard</a>, the niece of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_3rd_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas Howard, 3rd Duke of Norfolk">Thomas Howard</a>, the third <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Duke of Norfolk">Duke of Norfolk</a>. Catherine was promoted by Norfolk in the hope that she would persuade Henry to restore the Catholic religion in England. Henry called her his “rose without a thorn”, but the marriage ended in failure. Henry's fancy with Catherine started before the end of his marriage with Anne when she was still a member of Anne's court. Catherine was young and vivacious, but Henry's age made him less inclined to use Catherine in the bedroom; rather, he preferred to admire her, which Catherine soon grew tired of. Catherine, forced into a marriage to an unattractive, obese man over 30 years her senior, had never wanted to marry Henry, and conducted an affair with the King's favourite, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Culpeper" title="Thomas Culpeper">Thomas Culpeper</a>, while Henry and she were married. During her questioning, Catherine first denied everything but eventually she was broken down and told of her infidelity and her pre-nuptial relations with other men. Henry, first enraged, threatened to torture her to death but later became overcome with grief and self-pity. She was accused of treason and was <a href="/wiki/Execution" class="mw-redirect" title="Execution">executed</a> on 13 February 1542, destroying the English Catholic holdouts' hopes of a national reconciliation with the Catholic Church. Her execution also marked the end of the Howard family's power within the court.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By the time Henry conducted another Protestant marriage with his final wife <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Parr" title="Catherine Parr">Catherine Parr</a> in 1543, the old Roman Catholic advisers, including the powerful <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_3rd_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas Howard, 3rd Duke of Norfolk">third Duke of Norfolk</a>, had lost all their power and influence. The duke himself was still a committed Catholic, and he was nearly persuaded to arrest Catherine for preaching Lutheran doctrines to Henry while she attended his ill health. However, she managed to reconcile with the King after vowing that she had only argued about religion with him to take his mind off the suffering caused by his ulcerous leg. Her peacemaking also helped reconcile Henry with his daughters Mary and Elizabeth and fostered a good relationship between her and the crown prince. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_VI:_Protestant_zeal">Edward VI: Protestant zeal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Edward VI: Protestant zeal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Henry died on 28 January 1547. His <a href="/wiki/Will_(law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Will (law)">will</a> had reinstated his daughters by his annulled marriages to <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_British_Throne" class="mw-redirect" title="Succession to the British Throne">line of succession</a>. Edward, his nine-year-old son by <a href="/wiki/Jane_Seymour" title="Jane Seymour">Jane Seymour</a>, succeeded as <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI of England</a>. Unfortunately, the young King's kingdom was usually in turmoil between nobles who were trying to strengthen their own positions in the kingdom by using the Regency in their favour.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Duke_of_Somerset.27s_England"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Duke_of_Somerset's_England">Duke of Somerset's England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Duke of Somerset's England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Book_of_common_prayer_1549.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Book_of_common_prayer_1549.jpg/170px-Book_of_common_prayer_1549.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Book_of_common_prayer_1549.jpg/255px-Book_of_common_prayer_1549.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Book_of_common_prayer_1549.jpg/340px-Book_of_common_prayer_1549.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1296" data-file-height="2000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Book_of_common_prayer_1549.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The title page of Archbishop Cranmer's 
         <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>, 1549
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Although Henry had specified a group of men to act as regents during Edward's minority, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour</a>, Edward's uncle, quickly seized complete control and created himself <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Somerset" title="Duke of Somerset">Duke of Somerset</a> on 15 February 1547. His domination of the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a>, the king's most senior body of advisers, was unchallenged. Somerset aimed to unite England and Scotland by marrying Edward to the young <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, and aimed to forcibly impose the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>. Somerset led a large and well equipped army to Scotland, where he and the Scottish regent <a href="/wiki/James_Hamilton,_2nd_Earl_of_Arran" class="mw-redirect" title="James Hamilton, 2nd Earl of Arran">James Hamilton, 2nd Earl of Arran</a>, commanded their armies at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pinkie_Cleugh" title="Battle of Pinkie Cleugh">Battle of Pinkie Cleugh</a> on 10 September 1547. The English won the battle, and after this Queen Mary of Scotland was smuggled to France, where she was betrothed to the <a href="/wiki/Dauphin_of_France" title="Dauphin of France">Dauphin</a>, the future King <a href="/wiki/Francis_II_of_France" title="Francis II of France">Francis II of France</a>. Despite Somerset's disappointment that no Scottish marriage would take place, his victory at Pinkie Cleugh made his position appear unassailable.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Edward VI was taught that he had to lead religious reform. In 1549, the Crown ordered the publication of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>, containing the forms of worship for daily and Sunday church services. The controversial new book was not welcomed by either reformers or Catholic conservatives; it was especially condemned in <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, where traditional Catholic loyalty was at its strongest. In Cornwall at the time, many of the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_people" title="Cornish people">people</a> could only speak the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a>, so the uniform <a href="/wiki/English_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="English Bible">English Bibles</a> and church services were not understood by many. This caused the <a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_Rebellion" title="Prayer Book Rebellion">Prayer Book Rebellion</a>, in which groups of Cornish non-conformists gathered round the mayor. The rebellion worried Somerset, now <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a>, and he sent an army to impose a military solution to the rebellion. The rebellion hardened the Crown against Catholics. Fear of Catholicism focused on Edward's elder sister, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary</a>, who was a pious and devout Catholic. Although called before the Privy Council several times to renounce her faith and stop hearing the Catholic Mass, she refused. Edward had a good relationship with his sister <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth</a>, who was a Protestant, albeit a moderate one, but this was strained when Elizabeth was accused of having an affair with the Duke of Somerset's brother, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Seymour,_1st_Baron_Seymour_of_Sudeley" title="Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley">Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley</a>, the husband of Henry's last wife <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Parr" title="Catherine Parr">Catherine Parr</a>. Elizabeth was interviewed by one of Edward's advisers, and she was eventually found not to be guilty, despite forced confessions from her servants <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Champernowne" class="mw-redirect" title="Catherine Champernowne">Catherine Ashley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Parry_(Comptroller_of_the_Household)" title="Thomas Parry (Comptroller of the Household)">Thomas Parry</a>. Thomas Seymour was arrested and beheaded on 20 March 1549. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problematic_succession">Problematic succession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Problematic succession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VI_of_England_c._1546.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Edward_VI_of_England_c._1546.jpg/170px-Edward_VI_of_England_c._1546.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Edward_VI_of_England_c._1546.jpg/255px-Edward_VI_of_England_c._1546.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Edward_VI_of_England_c._1546.jpg/340px-Edward_VI_of_England_c._1546.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3041" data-file-height="4063"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VI_of_England_c._1546.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A small boy with a big mind: 
         <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI</a>, desperate for a Protestant succession, changed his father's will to allow 
         <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a> to become queen
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Somerset</a> was also losing favour. After forcibly removing Edward VI to <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>, with the intention of keeping him hostage, Somerset was removed from power by members of the council, led by his chief rival, <a href="/wiki/John_Dudley,_1st_Duke_of_Northumberland" title="John Dudley, 1st Duke of Northumberland">John Dudley</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Warwick" title="Earl of Warwick">Earl of Warwick</a>, who created himself <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Northumberland" title="Duke of Northumberland">Duke of Northumberland</a> shortly after his rise. Northumberland effectively became Lord Protector, but he did not use this title, learning from the mistakes his predecessor made. Northumberland was furiously ambitious, and aimed to secure Protestant uniformity while making himself rich with land and money in the process. He ordered churches to be stripped of all traditional Catholic symbolism, resulting in the simplicity often seen in <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> churches today. A revision of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> was published in 1552. When Edward VI became ill in 1553, his advisers looked to the possible imminent accession of the Catholic Lady Mary, and feared that she would overturn all the reforms made during Edward's reign. Perhaps surprisingly, it was the dying Edward himself who feared a return to Catholicism, and wrote a new <a href="/wiki/Will_(law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Will (law)">will</a> repudiating the 1544 will of Henry VIII. This gave the throne to his cousin <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a>, the granddaughter of Henry VIII's sister <a href="/wiki/Mary_Tudor_(queen_consort_of_France)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Tudor (queen consort of France)">Mary Tudor</a>, who, after the death of <a href="/wiki/Louis_XII_of_France" title="Louis XII of France">Louis XII of France</a> in 1515 had married Henry VIII's favourite <a href="/wiki/Charles_Brandon,_1st_Duke_of_Suffolk" title="Charles Brandon, 1st Duke of Suffolk">Charles Brandon</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Suffolk" title="Duke of Suffolk">Duke of Suffolk</a>. Lady Jane's mother was <a href="/wiki/Lady_Frances_Brandon" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Frances Brandon">Lady Frances Brandon</a>, the daughter of Suffolk and Princess Mary. Northumberland married Jane to his youngest son <a href="/wiki/Guildford_Dudley" class="mw-redirect" title="Guildford Dudley">Guildford Dudley</a>, allowing himself to get the most out of a necessary Protestant succession. Most of Edward's council signed the <i>Devise for the Succession</i>, and when Edward VI died on 6 July 1553 from his battle with tuberculosis, Lady Jane was proclaimed queen. However, the popular support for the rightful successor Mary – even though she was Catholic – overruled Northumberland's plans, and Jane, who had never wanted to accept the crown, was deposed after just nine days. Mary's supporters joined her in a triumphal procession to London, accompanied by her younger sister <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth</a>. </p>
      <p>With the death of Edward VI, the direct male line of the House of Tudor went extinct. </p> 
      <h2><span id="Mary_I:_A_troubled_queen.27s_reign"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mary_I:_A_troubled_queen's_reign">Mary I: A troubled queen's reign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mary I: A troubled queen's reign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Maria_Tudor1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Maria_Tudor1.jpg/170px-Maria_Tudor1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Maria_Tudor1.jpg/255px-Maria_Tudor1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Maria_Tudor1.jpg/340px-Maria_Tudor1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1434" data-file-height="1899"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Maria_Tudor1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I of England</a>, who tried to return England to the Roman Catholic Church
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Mary soon announced her intention to marry the Spanish prince <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip</a>, son of her mother's nephew <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor</a>. The prospect of a marriage alliance with Spain proved unpopular with the English people, who were worried that Spain would use England as a satellite, involving England in wars without the popular support of the people. Popular discontent grew; a Protestant courtier, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wyatt_the_younger" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Wyatt the younger">Thomas Wyatt the younger</a>, led a <a href="/wiki/Wyatt%27s_rebellion" title="Wyatt's rebellion">rebellion</a> against Mary aiming to depose and replace her with her half-sister <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth</a>. The plot was discovered, and Wyatt's supporters were hunted down and killed. Wyatt himself was tortured, in the hope that he would give evidence that Elizabeth was involved so that Mary could have her executed for treason. Wyatt never implicated Elizabeth, and he was <a href="/wiki/Decapitation" title="Decapitation">beheaded</a>. Elizabeth spent her time between different prisons, including the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>. </p>
      <p>Mary married Philip at <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Cathedral" title="Winchester Cathedral">Winchester Cathedral</a>, on 25 July 1554. Philip found her unattractive, and only spent a minimal amount of time with her. Despite Mary believing she was pregnant numerous times during her five-year reign, she never reproduced. Devastated that she rarely saw her husband, and anxious that she was not bearing an heir to Catholic England, Mary became bitter. In her determination to restore England to the Catholic faith and to secure her throne from Protestant threats, she had 200-300 Protestants burnt at the stake in the <a href="/wiki/Marian_Persecutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Marian Persecutions">Marian Persecutions</a> between 1555 and 1558. Protestants came to hate her as "Bloody Mary." <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> stated that "as bloody Queen Mary this woman has become famous, and as Bloody Queen Mary she will ever be remembered with horror and detestation"<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Latimer_Ridley_Foxe_burning.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Latimer_Ridley_Foxe_burning.jpg/220px-Latimer_Ridley_Foxe_burning.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Latimer_Ridley_Foxe_burning.jpg/330px-Latimer_Ridley_Foxe_burning.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Latimer_Ridley_Foxe_burning.jpg/440px-Latimer_Ridley_Foxe_burning.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1546" data-file-height="1297"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Latimer_Ridley_Foxe_burning.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Protestants 
         <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Latimer" title="Hugh Latimer">Hugh Latimer</a> and 
         <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Ridley_(martyr)" title="Nicholas Ridley (martyr)">Nicholas Ridley</a> being burned at the stake during Mary's reign
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Mary's dream of a new, Catholic <a href="/wiki/Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a> line was finished, and her popularity further declined when she lost the last English area on French soil, <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Francis,_Duke_of_Guise" title="Francis, Duke of Guise">Francis, Duke of Guise</a>, on 7 January 1558. Mary's reign, however, introduced a new coining system that would be used until the 18th century, and her marriage to Philip II created new trade routes for England. Mary's government took a number of steps towards reversing the inflation, budgetary deficits, poverty, and trade crisis of her kingdom. She explored the commercial potential of Russian, African, and Baltic markets, revised the customs system, worked to counter the currency debasements of her predecessors, amalgamated several revenue courts, and strengthened the governing authority of the middling and larger towns.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Mary also welcomed the first Russian ambassador to England, creating relations between England and Russia for the first time. Had she lived a little longer, Catholicism, which she worked so hard to restore into the realm might have taken deeper roots than it did. However, her actions in pursuit of this goal arguably spurred on the Protestant cause, through the many martyrs she made. Mary died on 17 November 1558 at the relatively young age of 42.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elizabeth_I:_Age_of_intrigues_and_plots">Elizabeth I: Age of intrigues and plots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Elizabeth I: Age of intrigues and plots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/170px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/255px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/340px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2863" data-file-height="3845"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a> at her 
         <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_monarch" title="Coronation of the British monarch">coronation</a> on 15 January 1559
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Elizabeth I, who was staying at <a href="/wiki/Hatfield_House" title="Hatfield House">Hatfield House</a> at the time of her accession, rode to London to the cheers of both the ruling class and the common people. </p>
      <p>When Elizabeth came to the throne, there was much apprehension among members of the council appointed by Mary, because many of them (as noted by the Spanish ambassador) had participated in several plots against Elizabeth, such as her imprisonment in the Tower, trying to force her to marry a foreign prince and thereby sending her out of the realm, and even pushing for her death.<sup id="cite_ref-JonesNorman_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JonesNorman-22">[22]</a></sup> In response to their fear, she chose as her chief minister <a href="/wiki/William_Cecil,_1st_Baron_Burghley" title="William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley">Sir William Cecil</a>, a Protestant, and former secretary to Lord Protector the <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Duke of Somerset</a> and then to the <a href="/wiki/John_Dudley,_1st_Duke_of_Northumberland" title="John Dudley, 1st Duke of Northumberland">Duke of Northumberland</a>. Under Mary, he had been spared, and often visited Elizabeth, ostensibly to review her accounts and expenditure. Elizabeth also appointed her personal favourite, the son of the Duke of Northumberland <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dudley,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester">Lord Robert Dudley</a>, her <a href="/wiki/Master_of_the_Horse#Master_of_the_Horse_(United_Kingdom)" title="Master of the Horse">Master of the Horse</a>, giving him constant personal access to the queen. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Elizabeth had a long, turbulent path to the throne. She had a number of problems during her childhood, one of the main ones being after the execution of her mother, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>. When Anne was beheaded, Henry declared Elizabeth an illegitimate child and she would, therefore, not be able to inherit the throne. After the death of her father, she was raised by his widow, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Parr" title="Catherine Parr">Catherine Parr</a> and her husband <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Seymour,_1st_Baron_Seymour_of_Sudeley" title="Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley">Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley</a>. A scandal arose with her and the Lord Admiral to which she stood trial. During the examinations, she answered truthfully and boldly and all charges were dropped. She was an excellent student, well-schooled in Latin, French, Italian, and somewhat in Greek, and was a talented writer.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> She was supposedly a very skilled musician as well, in both singing and playing the lute. After the rebellion of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wyatt_the_younger" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Wyatt the younger">Thomas Wyatt the younger</a>, Elizabeth was imprisoned in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>. No proof could be found that Elizabeth was involved and she was released and retired to the countryside until the death of her sister, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imposing_the_Church_of_England">Imposing the Church of England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Imposing the Church of England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Elizabeth was a moderate Protestant; she was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>, who played a key role in the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> in the 1520s. She had been brought up by Blanche Herbert <a href="/wiki/Lady_Troy" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Troy">Lady Troy</a>. At her <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_Monarch" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of the British Monarch">coronation</a> in January 1559, many of the bishops – Catholic, appointed by Mary, who had expelled many of the Protestant clergymen when she became queen in 1553 – refused to perform the service in English. Eventually, the relatively minor <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Carlisle" title="Bishop of Carlisle">Bishop of Carlisle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Owen_Oglethorpe" title="Owen Oglethorpe">Owen Oglethorpe</a>, performed the ceremony; but when Oglethorpe attempted to perform traditional Catholic parts of the Coronation, Elizabeth got up and left. Following the Coronation, two important Acts were passed through parliament: the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1559" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Uniformity 1559">Act of Uniformity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy_1559" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Supremacy 1559">Act of Supremacy</a>, establishing the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> and creating Elizabeth <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Governor of the Church of England</a> (<i>Supreme Head</i>, the title used by her father and brother, was seen as inappropriate for a woman ruler). These acts, known collectively as the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a>, made it compulsory to attend church services every Sunday; and imposed an oath on clergymen and statesmen to recognise the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, the independence of the Church of England from the Catholic Church, and the authority of Elizabeth as Supreme Governor. Elizabeth made it clear that if they refused the oath the first time, they would have a second opportunity, after which, if the oath was not sworn, the offenders would be deprived of their offices and estates. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pressure_to_marry">Pressure to marry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pressure to marry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mary,_Queen_of_Scots_after_Nicholas_Hilliard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Mary%2C_Queen_of_Scots_after_Nicholas_Hilliard.jpg/170px-Mary%2C_Queen_of_Scots_after_Nicholas_Hilliard.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Mary%2C_Queen_of_Scots_after_Nicholas_Hilliard.jpg/255px-Mary%2C_Queen_of_Scots_after_Nicholas_Hilliard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Mary%2C_Queen_of_Scots_after_Nicholas_Hilliard.jpg/340px-Mary%2C_Queen_of_Scots_after_Nicholas_Hilliard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2521"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mary,_Queen_of_Scots_after_Nicholas_Hilliard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, who conspired with English nobles to take the English throne for herself
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Even though Elizabeth was only twenty-five when she came to the throne, she was absolutely sure of her God-given place to be the queen and of her responsibilities as the 'handmaiden of the Lord'. She never let anyone challenge her authority as queen, even though many people, who felt she was weak and should be married, tried to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-JonesNorman_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JonesNorman-22">[22]</a></sup> The popularity of Elizabeth was extremely high, but her <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a>, her <a href="/wiki/Houses_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Houses of Parliament">Parliament</a> and her subjects thought that the unmarried queen should take a husband; it was generally accepted that, once a <a href="/wiki/Queen_regnant" title="Queen regnant">queen regnant</a> was married, the husband would relieve the woman of the burdens of <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>. Also, without an heir, the Tudor line would end; the risk of civil war between rival claimants was a possibility if Elizabeth died childless. Numerous suitors from nearly all European nations sent ambassadors to English court to put forward their suit. Risk of death came dangerously close in 1564 when Elizabeth caught <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>; when she was most at risk, she named <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dudley,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester">Robert Dudley</a> as Lord Protector in the event of her death. After her recovery, she appointed Dudley to the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a> and created him <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Leicester" title="Earl of Leicester">Earl of Leicester</a>, in the hope that he would marry <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>. Mary rejected him, and instead married <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stuart,_Lord_Darnley" title="Henry Stuart, Lord Darnley">Henry Stuart, Lord Darnley</a>, a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a>, giving Mary a stronger claim to the English throne. Although many Catholics were loyal to Elizabeth, many also believed that, because Elizabeth was declared illegitimate after her parents' marriage was <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulled</a>, Mary was the strongest legitimate claimant. Despite this, Elizabeth would not name Mary her heir; as she had experienced during the reign of her predecessor Mary I, the opposition could flock around the heir if they were disheartened with Elizabeth's rule. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:El_Greco_050.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/El_Greco_050.jpg/170px-El_Greco_050.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/El_Greco_050.jpg/255px-El_Greco_050.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/El_Greco_050.jpg/340px-El_Greco_050.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1576" data-file-height="2119"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:El_Greco_050.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a>, who issued the 
         <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">Papal bull</a> excommunicating Elizabeth and relieving her subjects of their allegiance to her
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Numerous threats to the Tudor line occurred during Elizabeth's reign. In 1569, a group of Earls led by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Neville,_6th_Earl_of_Westmorland" title="Charles Neville, 6th Earl of Westmorland">Charles Neville</a>, the sixth <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Westmorland" title="Earl of Westmorland">Earl of Westmorland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Percy,_7th_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Thomas Percy, 7th Earl of Northumberland">Thomas Percy</a>, the seventh <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Earl of Northumberland">Earl of Northumberland</a> attempted to depose Elizabeth and replace her with <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>. In 1571, the Protestant-turned-Catholic <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_4th_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas Howard, 4th Duke of Norfolk">Thomas Howard</a>, the fourth <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Duke of Norfolk">Duke of Norfolk</a>, had plans to marry Mary, Queen of Scots, and then replace Elizabeth with Mary. <a href="/wiki/Ridolfi_plot" title="Ridolfi plot">The plot</a>, masterminded by <a href="/wiki/Roberto_di_Ridolfi" title="Roberto di Ridolfi">Roberto di Ridolfi</a>, was discovered and Norfolk was <a href="/wiki/Decapitation" title="Decapitation">beheaded</a>. The next major uprising was in 1601, when <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_2nd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex">Robert Devereux</a>, the second <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Essex" title="Earl of Essex">Earl of Essex</a>, attempted to raise the city of London against Elizabeth's government. The city of London proved unwilling to rebel; Essex and most of his co-rebels were executed. Threats also came from abroad. In 1570, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a> issued a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">Papal bull</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Regnans_in_Excelsis" title="Regnans in Excelsis">Regnans in Excelsis</a></i>, excommunicating Elizabeth, and releasing her subjects from their <a href="/wiki/Allegiance" title="Allegiance">allegiance</a> to her. Elizabeth came under pressure from <a href="/wiki/UK_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Parliament">Parliament</a> to execute Mary, Queen of Scots, to prevent any further attempts to replace her; though faced with several official requests, she vacillated over the decision to execute an anointed queen. Finally, she was persuaded of Mary's (treasonous) complicity in the plotting against her, and she signed the <a href="/wiki/Execution_warrant" title="Execution warrant">death warrant</a> in 1586. Mary was executed at <a href="/wiki/Fotheringay_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Fotheringay Castle">Fotheringay Castle</a> on 8 February 1587, to the outrage of Catholic Europe. </p>
      <p>There are many reasons debated as to why Elizabeth never married. It was rumoured that she was in love with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dudley,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester">Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester</a>, and that on one of her summer progresses she had birthed his illegitimate child. This rumour was just one of many that swirled around the two's long-standing friendship. However, more important to focus on were the disasters that many women, such as <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a>, suffered due to being married into the royal family. Her sister Mary's marriage to Philip brought great contempt to the country, for many of her subjects despised Spain and Philip and feared that he would try to take complete control. Recalling her father's disdain for <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Cleves" title="Anne of Cleves">Anne of Cleves</a>, Elizabeth also refused to enter into a foreign match with a man that she had never seen before, so that also eliminated a large number of suitors.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_hopes_of_a_Tudor_heir">Last hopes of a Tudor heir</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Last hopes of a Tudor heir">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Despite the uncertainty of Elizabeth's – and therefore the Tudors' – hold on England, she never married. The closest she came to marriage was between 1579 and 1581, when she was courted by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois,_Duc_d%27Anjou" class="mw-redirect" title="François, Duc d'Anjou">Francis, Duke of Anjou</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_France" title="Henry II of France">Henry II of France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catherine_de%27_Medici" title="Catherine de' Medici">Catherine de' Medici</a>. Despite Elizabeth's government constantly begging her to marry in the early years of her reign, it was now persuading Elizabeth not to marry the French prince, for his mother, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_de%27_Medici" title="Catherine de' Medici">Catherine de' Medici</a>, was suspected of ordering the <a href="/wiki/St_Bartholomew%27s_Day_massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="St Bartholomew's Day massacre">St Bartholomew's Day massacre</a> of tens of thousands of French Protestant <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenots</a> in 1572. Elizabeth bowed to public feeling against the marriage, learning from the mistake her sister made when she married <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II of Spain</a>, and sent the Duke of Anjou away. Elizabeth knew that the continuation of the Tudor line was now impossible; she was forty-eight in 1581, and too old to bear children. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Loutherbourg-Spanish_Armada.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Loutherbourg-Spanish_Armada.jpg/220px-Loutherbourg-Spanish_Armada.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Loutherbourg-Spanish_Armada.jpg/330px-Loutherbourg-Spanish_Armada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Loutherbourg-Spanish_Armada.jpg/440px-Loutherbourg-Spanish_Armada.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1263" data-file-height="971"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Loutherbourg-Spanish_Armada.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">The Spanish Armada</a>: Catholic Spain's attempt to depose Elizabeth and take control of England
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>By far the most dangerous threat to the Tudor line during Elizabeth's reign was the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> of 1588, launched by Elizabeth's old suitor <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II of Spain</a> and commanded by <a href="/wiki/Alonso_de_Guzm%C3%A1n_El_Bueno,_7th_Duke_of_Medina_Sidonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Alonso de Guzmán El Bueno, 7th Duke of Medina Sidonia">Alonso de Guzmán El Bueno</a>, the seventh <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Medina_Sidonia" title="Duke of Medina Sidonia">Duke of Medina Sidonia</a>. The Spanish invasion fleet outnumbered the English fleet's 22 <a href="/wiki/Galleons" class="mw-redirect" title="Galleons">galleons</a> and 108 armed merchant ships. The Spanish lost, however, as a result of bad weather on the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>, poor planning and logistics, and the skills of <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Sir Francis Drake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Howard,_1st_Earl_of_Nottingham" title="Charles Howard, 1st Earl of Nottingham">Charles Howard</a>, the second <a href="/wiki/Baron_Howard_of_Effingham" class="mw-redirect" title="Baron Howard of Effingham">Baron Howard of Effingham</a> (later first <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Nottingham" title="Earl of Nottingham">Earl of Nottingham</a>). </p>
      <p>While Elizabeth declined physically with age, her running of the country continued to benefit her people.
