In response to famine across England due to bad harvests in the 1590s, Elizabeth introduced the <a href="/wiki/Poor_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor law">poor law</a>, allowing peasants who were too ill to work a certain amount of money from the state. All the money Elizabeth had borrowed from Parliament in 12 of the 13 parliamentary sessions was paid back; by the time of her death, Elizabeth not only had no debts, but was in credit. Elizabeth died childless at <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Palace" title="Richmond Palace">Richmond Palace</a> on 24 March 1603. She left behind a legacy and monarchy worth noting. She had pursued her goals of being well endowed with every aspect of ruling her kingdom, and of knowing everything necessary to be an effective monarch. She took part in law, economics, politics and governmental issues both domestic and abroad. Realms that had once been strictly forbidden to the female gender had now been ruled by one. </p>
      <p>Elizabeth never named a successor. However, her chief minister Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_1st_Earl_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Cecil, 1st Earl of Salisbury">Robert Cecil</a> had corresponded with the Protestant <a href="/wiki/King_James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="King James VI of Scotland">King James VI of Scotland</a>, great-grandson of <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret Tudor</a>, and James's succession to the English throne was unopposed. There has been discussion over the selected heir. It has been argued that Elizabeth would have selected James because she felt guilty about what happened to his mother, her cousin. Whether this is true is unknown for certain, for Elizabeth did her best to never show emotion nor give in to claims. Elizabeth was strong and hard-headed and kept her primary goal in sight: providing the best for her people and proving those wrong who doubted her while maintaining a straight composure. </p>
      <p>The House of Tudor survives through the female line, first with the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a>, which occupied the English throne for most of the following century, and then the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">House of Hanover</a>, via James' granddaughter <a href="/wiki/Sophia,_Electress_of_Hanover" class="mw-redirect" title="Sophia, Electress of Hanover">Sophia</a>. Queen Elizabeth II is a direct descendant of Henry VII. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_and_after_comparisons">Before and after comparisons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Before and after comparisons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a>
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">
           This section 
           <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.
           <span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> 
           <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2018</span>)</i></small>
           <small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>Public interference regarding the Roses dynasties was always a threat until the 17th century Stuart/Bourbon re-alignment occasioned by a series of events such as the execution of <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a>, despite her brother in law, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dudley,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester#Governor-General_of_the_United_Provinces" title="Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester">Leicester's reputation in Holland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rising_of_the_North" title="Rising of the North">Rising of the North</a> (in which the old <a href="/wiki/Percy-Neville_feud" class="mw-redirect" title="Percy-Neville feud">Percy-Neville feud</a> and even anti-Scottish sentiment was discarded on account of religion; <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">Northern England</a> shared the same Avignonese bias as the Scottish court, on par with Valois France and Castile, which became the backbone of the Counter-Reformation, with Protestants being solidly anti-Avignonese) and death of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I of England</a> without children. </p>
      <p>The Tudors made no substantial changes in their foreign policy from either Lancaster or York, whether the alliance was with Aragon or Cleves, the chief foreign enemies continuing as the <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a>, but the Tudors resurrected old ecclesiastic arguments once pursued by <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II of England</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/John_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="John of England">John of England</a>. Yorkists were tied so much to the old order that Catholic rebellions (such as the <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage_of_Grace" title="Pilgrimage of Grace">Pilgrimage of Grace</a>) and aspirations (exemplified by <a href="/wiki/William_Allen_(cardinal)" title="William Allen (cardinal)">William Allen</a>) were seen as continuing in their reactionary footsteps, when in opposition to the Tudors' reformation policies, although the Tudors were not uniformly Protestant according to Continental definition—instead were true to their <a href="/wiki/Henry_Beaufort" title="Henry Beaufort">Lancastrian Beaufort</a> allegiance, in the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Pole" title="Reginald Pole">Reginald Pole</a>. </p>
      <p>The essential difference between the Tudors and their predecessors, is the nationalization and integration of <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>'s ideas to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, holding onto the alignment of <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Bohemia" title="Anne of Bohemia">Anne of Bohemia</a>, in which Anne's <a href="/wiki/Hussite" class="mw-redirect" title="Hussite">Hussite</a> brethren were in alliance to her husband's Wycliffite countrymen against the <a href="/wiki/Avignon_Papacy" title="Avignon Papacy">Avignon Papacy</a>. The Tudors otherwise rejected or suppressed other religious notions, whether for the Pope's award of <i>Fidei Defensor</i> or to prevent them from being in the hands of the common laity, who might be swayed by cells of foreign Protestants, with whom they had conversation as <a href="/wiki/Marian_exiles" title="Marian exiles">Marian exiles</a>, pursuing a strategy of containment which the Lancastrians had done (after being vilified by <a href="/wiki/Wat_Tyler" title="Wat Tyler">Wat Tyler</a>), even though the phenomenon of "<a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollard knights</a>" (like <a href="/wiki/John_Oldcastle" title="John Oldcastle">John Oldcastle</a>) had become almost a national sensation all on its own. </p>
      <p>In essence, the Tudors followed a composite of Lancastrian (the court party) and Yorkist (the church party) policies. Henry VIII tried to extend his father's balancing act between the dynasties for opportunistic interventionism in the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Wars" title="Italian Wars">Italian Wars</a>, which had unfortunate consequences for his own marriages and the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a>; the King furthermore tried to use similar tactics for the "via media" concept of <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>. A further parallelism was effected by turning Ireland into a kingdom and sharing the same episcopal establishment as England, whilst enlarging England by the annexation of Wales. The progress to Northern/Roses government would thenceforth pass across the border into Scotland, in 1603, due not only to the civil warring, but also because the Tudors' own line was fragile and insecure, trying to reconcile the mortal enemies who had weakened England to the point of having to bow to new pressures, rather than dictate diplomacy on English terms. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellions_against_the_Tudors">Rebellions against the Tudors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Rebellions against the Tudors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The following English rebellions took place against the House of Tudor: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li><i>Yorkist risings against Henry VII</i> (1486–1487)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227-27">[27]</a></sup> 
        <ul>
         <li>The first was the Rebellion of the <a href="/wiki/Stafford_and_Lovell_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Stafford and Lovell Rebellion">Stafford brothers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Lovell,_1st_Viscount_Lovell" title="Francis Lovell, 1st Viscount Lovell">Viscount Lovell</a> of 1486, which collapsed without fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrimes199969_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrimes199969-28">[28]</a></sup></li> 
         <li>In 1487, Yorkists led by <a href="/wiki/John_de_la_Pole,_1st_Earl_of_Lincoln" title="John de la Pole, 1st Earl of Lincoln">John, Earl of Lincoln</a> rebelled in support of <a href="/wiki/Lambert_Simnel" title="Lambert Simnel">Lambert Simnel</a>, a boy who was claimed to be the <a href="/wiki/Edward_Plantagenet,_17th_Earl_of_Warwick" title="Edward Plantagenet, 17th Earl of Warwick">Earl of Warwick</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrimes199972_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrimes199972-29">[29]</a></sup> son of Edward IV's brother <a href="/wiki/George_Plantagenet,_1st_Duke_of_Clarence" title="George Plantagenet, 1st Duke of Clarence">Clarence</a> (who had last been seen as a prisoner in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower</a>). The rebellion began in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, where the traditionally <a href="/wiki/Yorkist" class="mw-redirect" title="Yorkist">Yorkist</a> nobility, headed by the powerful <a href="/wiki/Gerald_FitzGerald,_8th_Earl_of_Kildare" title="Gerald FitzGerald, 8th Earl of Kildare">Gerald, Earl of Kildare</a>, proclaimed Simnel King and provided troops for his invasion of England. The rebellion was defeated and Lincoln killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stoke" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Stoke">Battle of Stoke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams197362_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams197362-30">[30]</a></sup></li> 
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_rebellion_1489" title="Yorkshire rebellion 1489">Yorkshire Rebellion</a></i> (1489)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227-27">[27]</a></sup> — Rioting led by Sir <a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Egremont&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="John Egremont (page does not exist)">John Egremont</a> was suppressed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_2nd_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas Howard, 2nd Duke of Norfolk">Thomas, Earl of Surrey</a> but not before <a href="/wiki/Henry_Percy,_4th_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Henry Percy, 4th Earl of Northumberland">Henry, Earl of Northumberland</a> was killed collecting taxes for the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_%C3%89taples" title="Peace of Étaples">War in Brittany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227-27">[27]</a></sup></li> 
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Cornish_Rebellion_of_1497" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish Rebellion of 1497">Cornish Rebellion</a></i> (1497)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227-27">[27]</a></sup></li> 
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Second_Cornish_Uprising_of_1497" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Cornish Uprising of 1497">Second Cornish Uprising of 1497</a></i> — <a href="/wiki/Perkin_Warbeck" title="Perkin Warbeck">Perkin Warbeck</a>, who claimed to be <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Shrewsbury,_1st_Duke_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard of Shrewsbury, 1st Duke of York">Richard</a>, the younger of the "Princes in the Tower", landed in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> with a few thousand troops, but was soon captured and executed in 1499.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrimes199969–70_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrimes199969–70-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
        </ul></li> 
       <li><i>Rebellions against Henry VIII</i> 
        <ul>
         <li><i>The <a href="/wiki/Amicable_Grant_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Amicable Grant Rebellion">Amicable Grant Rebellion</a></i> (1525)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227-27">[27]</a></sup></li> 
         <li><i>The <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage_of_Grace" title="Pilgrimage of Grace">Pilgrimage of Grace</a></i> (1536)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227-27">[27]</a></sup></li>
        </ul></li> 
       <li><i>Rebellions against Edward VI's "protectors"</i> 
        <ul>
         <li><i>The <a href="/wiki/Western_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Rebellion">Western Rebellion</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_Rebellion" title="Prayer Book Rebellion">Prayer Book Rebellion</a></i> (1549)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228-32">[32]</a></sup></li> 
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Kett%27s_Rebellion" title="Kett's Rebellion">Kett's Rebellion</a></i> (1549)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
        </ul></li> 
       <li><i>Rebellions against Mary I</i> 
        <ul>
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Wyatt%27s_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Wyatt's Rebellion">Wyatt's Rebellion</a></i> (1554)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
        </ul></li> 
       <li><i>Rebellions against Elizabeth I</i> 
        <ul>
         <li><i>The <a href="/wiki/Rebellion_of_the_Northern_Earls" class="mw-redirect" title="Rebellion of the Northern Earls">Rebellion of the Northern Earls</a></i> (1569)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228-32">[32]</a></sup></li> 
         <li><i>The <a href="/wiki/Essex_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Essex Rebellion">Essex Rebellion</a></i> (1601)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
        </ul></li>
      </ul> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tudor_monarchs_of_England_and_Ireland">Tudor monarchs of England and Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Tudor monarchs of England and Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

         <td>Daughter of Henry VIII and Catherine of Aragon; known as "Bloody Mary" for burning Protestants during her reign. </td>

       <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tudor_Badges">Tudor Badges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Tudor Badges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
       <p>The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Dragon" title="Welsh Dragon">Welsh Dragon</a> supporter honoured the Tudor's Welsh origins. The most popular symbol of the house of Tudor was the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor rose</a> (see top of page). When <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry Tudor</a> took the crown of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> from <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III</a> in battle, he brought about the end of the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a> between the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">House of Lancaster</a> (whose badge was a red rose) and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_York" title="House of York">House of York</a> (whose badge was a white rose). He married <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_York" title="Elizabeth of York">Elizabeth of York</a> to bring all factions together. On his marriage, Henry adopted the Tudor Rose badge conjoining the <a href="/wiki/White_Rose_of_York" title="White Rose of York">White Rose of York</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Rose_of_Lancaster" title="Red Rose of Lancaster">Red Rose of Lancaster</a>. It symbolized the Tudor's right to rule as well the uniting of the kingdom after the Wars of the Roses. It was used by every English, then British, monarch since Henry VII as a royal badge.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
       <table border="0" align="center" width="100%"> 
        <tbody>
         <tr>

       <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lineage_and_the_Tudor_name">Lineage and the Tudor name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Lineage and the Tudor name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
       <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Tudor_Name">The Tudor Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: The Tudor Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
       <p>As noted above <a href="/wiki/Tudor_(name)" title="Tudor (name)">Tewdur or Tudor</a> is derived from the words tud "territory" and rhi "king". Owen Tudor took it as a surname on being knighted. It is doubtful whether the Tudor kings used the name on the throne. Kings and princes were not seen as needing a name, and a " 'Tudor' name for the royal family was hardly known in the sixteenth century. The royal surname was never used in official publications, and hardly in ‘histories’ of various sorts before 1584. ... Monarchs were not anxious to publicize their descent in the paternal line from a Welsh adventurer, stressing instead continuity with the historic English and French royal families. Their subjects did not think of them as ‘Tudors’, or of themselves as ‘Tudor people’".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> Princes and Princesses would have been known as "of England". The medieval practice of colloquially calling princes after their place birth (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry of Bolingbroke</a> for Henry IV or <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry of Monmouth</a> for Henry V) was not followed. <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a> was likely known as "Henry of Richmond" before his taking of the throne. When Richard III called him "Henry Tudor" it was to stress his Welshness and his unfitness for the throne as opposed to himself, "Richard Plantagenet", a "true" descendant of the royal line. </p> 
       <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patrilineal_descent">Patrilineal descent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Patrilineal descent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

       <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_lineage">Royal lineage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Royal lineage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
       <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
        See also: 
        <a href="/wiki/English_monarchs_family_tree#House_of_Tudor" class="mw-redirect" title="English monarchs family tree">Tudor monarchs' family tree</a>
       </div> 
       <p>The Tudors' claim to the throne combined the Lancastrian claim in their descent from the Beauforts and the Yorkist claim by the marriage of Henry VII to the heiress of Edward IV. </p> 
       <table class="collapsible collapsed" style="margin: 0.3em auto auto; clear:none; min-width:60em; width:auto; font-size:88%; border:1px solid #aaa;"> 
        <tbody>
         <tr> 
          <th style="padding:0.2em 0.3em 0.2em 4.3em;background:none; width:auto">Royal Lineage (Simplified) </th>
         </tr> 
         <tr> 
          <td style="text-align:center"> 
           <div> 
            <table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:auto;">
             <tbody>
              <tr> 
               <td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;"> 
                <dl>
                 <dd>
                  <img alt="King of England" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Simple_gold_crown.svg/20px-Simple_gold_crown.svg.png" decoding="async" title="King of England" width="20" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Simple_gold_crown.svg/30px-Simple_gold_crown.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Simple_gold_crown.svg/40px-Simple_gold_crown.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128"> - King of England
                 </dd> 
                 <dd>
                  <img alt="House of Lancaster" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Red_Rose_Badge_of_Lancaster.svg/20px-Red_Rose_Badge_of_Lancaster.svg.png" decoding="async" title="House of Lancaster" width="20" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Red_Rose_Badge_of_Lancaster.svg/30px-Red_Rose_Badge_of_Lancaster.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Red_Rose_Badge_of_Lancaster.svg/40px-Red_Rose_Badge_of_Lancaster.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="250"> - House of Lancaster
                 </dd>
                </dl> <p> </p> </td> 
               <td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;"> 
                <dl>
                 <dd>
                  <img alt="House of York" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Yorkshire_rose.svg/20px-Yorkshire_rose.svg.png" decoding="async" title="House of York" width="20" height="19" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Yorkshire_rose.svg/30px-Yorkshire_rose.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Yorkshire_rose.svg/40px-Yorkshire_rose.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="276" data-file-height="264"> - House of York
                 </dd> 
                 <dd>
                  <img alt="House of Tudor" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Tudor_Rose.svg/20px-Tudor_Rose.svg.png" decoding="async" title="House of Tudor" width="20" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Tudor_Rose.svg/30px-Tudor_Rose.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Tudor_Rose.svg/40px-Tudor_Rose.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="250"> - House of Tudor
                 </dd>
                </dl> <p> </p> </td>

       <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Tudor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
       <p>Numerous feature films are based on Tudor history.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[35]</a></sup> Queen Elizabeth has been in special favorite for filmmakers for generations. According to Elizabeth A. Ford and Deborah C. Mitchell, images of Elizabeth I move: </p> 
       <dl>
        <dd>
         fast-forward across film history, unforgettable, iconic images: the stately bearing; the red wigs; the high forehead; the long, aristocratic nose; the alabaster makeup; the pearl-drop earrings; the stiff, ornate ruffs; the fingers dripping with jewels; and the gowns, with yards and yards of white satin, purple velvet, gold, and silver ornamented and sparkling with rubies, diamonds, and more pearls. Even a schoolchild would be hard-pressed to mistake her for any other monarch.
         <sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[36]</a></sup>
        </dd>
       </dl>

"Why Elizabeth I Never Married". <i>History Review</i> (67): 15–20.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=History+Review&amp;rft.atitle=Why+Elizabeth+I+Never+Married&amp;rft.issue=67&amp;rft.pages=15-20&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.aulast=Warnicke&amp;rft.aufirst=Retha&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHouse+of+Tudor" class="Z3988"></span>
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201227_27-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFO'Day2012">O'Day 2012</a>, p.&nbsp;27.</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrimes199969-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrimes199969_28-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFChrimes1999">Chrimes 1999</a>, p.&nbsp;69.</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrimes199972-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrimes199972_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFChrimes1999">Chrimes 1999</a>, p.&nbsp;72.</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams197362-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams197362_30-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWilliams1973">Williams 1973</a>, p.&nbsp;62.</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrimes199969–70-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrimes199969–70_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFChrimes1999">Chrimes 1999</a>, pp.&nbsp;69–70.</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Day201228_32-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFO'Day2012">O'Day 2012</a>, p.&nbsp;28.</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> <cite class="citation journal">Davies, C.S.L. (25 January 2012). "Tudor: What's in a Name?". <i>History</i>. <b>97</b> (325): 24–42. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1468-229X.2011.00540.x">10.1111/j.1468-229X.2011.00540.x</a>. <q>The ‘Tudor’ name for the royal family was hardly known in the sixteenth century. The almost obsessive use of the term by historians is therefore profoundly misleading about how English people of the time thought of themselves and of their world, the more so given the overtones of glamour associated with it. The royal surname was never used in official publications, and hardly in ‘histories’ of various sorts before 1584. Monarchs were not anxious to publicize their descent in the paternal line from a Welsh adventurer, stressing instead continuity with the historic English and French royal families. Their subjects did not think of them as ‘Tudors’, or of themselves as ‘Tudor people’. Modern concepts such as ‘Tudor monarchy’ are misleading in suggesting a false unity over the century. Subjects did not identify with their rulers in the way ‘Tudor people’ suggests. Nor did they situate themselves in a distinct ‘Tudor’ period of history, differentiated from a hypothetical ‘middle ages’. While ‘Tudor’ is useful historian's shorthand we should use the word sparingly and above all make clear to readers that it was not a contemporary concept.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=History&amp;rft.atitle=Tudor%3A+What%27s+in+a+Name%3F&amp;rft.volume=97&amp;rft.issue=325&amp;rft.pages=24-42&amp;rft.date=2012-01-25&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fj.1468-229X.2011.00540.x&amp;rft.aulast=Davies&amp;rft.aufirst=C.S.L.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHouse+of+Tudor" class="Z3988"></span>
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For an annotated list see <cite class="citation book">John A. Wagner; Susan Walters Schmid (2012). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=EUCY3otvttEC&amp;pg=PA1237"><i>Encyclopedia of Tudor England</i></a>. ABC-CLIO. p.&nbsp;1237ff. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781598842982" title="Special:BookSources/9781598842982"><bdi>9781598842982</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopedia+of+Tudor+England&amp;rft.pages=1237ff&amp;rft.pub=ABC-CLIO&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=9781598842982&amp;rft.au=John+A.+Wagner&amp;rft.au=Susan+Walters+Schmid&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DEUCY3otvttEC%26pg%3DPA1237&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHouse+of+Tudor" class="Z3988"></span>
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sarah Bruce, <i>The Henry VIII of England Handbook - Everything You Need To Know About Henry VIII of England</i> (2016) pp 119, 133, 152, 190-91.</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Elizabeth A. Ford and Deborah C. Mitchell, <i>Royal Portraits in Hollywood: Filming the Lives of Queens</i> (2009) pp 226-94 and see pp 126-56 For Mary Queen of Scots.</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Terry Deary, <i>Horrible Histories: Terrible Tudors</i> (Scholastic Australia, 2012).</span> </li> 
        </ol>
